/></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b> Phylogenetic analysis of <i>T. sinensis</i> and other sequenced plants. <i>O. sativa</i> was used as an outgroup. <b>b</b> Venn diagram of shared orthologous gene families in <i>T. sinensis</i>, <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>T. cacao</i>, and <i>C. sinensis</i>. <b>c</b> Density distributions of the synonymous substitution rate (Ks) between duplicated genes of syntenic regions in <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>P. trichocarpa</i>, <i>T. cacao</i>, and <i>T. sinensis</i> are shown by different colors. <b>d</b> Phylogenetic tree and expansion and contraction of gene families. The phylogenetic tree was constructed from a concatenated alignment of 509 families of single-copy genes from 18 higher plant species. Sly, <i>S. lycopersicum</i>; Fsc, <i>F. chinensis</i>; Oue<i>, O. europaea;</i> Csi <i>C. sinensis</i>; Ath<i>, A. thaliana;</i> Cpa<i>, C. papaya;</i> Gra<i>, G. raimondii</i>; Tca<i>, T. cacao;</i> Tsi, <i>T. sinensis;</i> Jcu<i>, J. curcas</i>; Ptr, <i>P. trichocarpa</i>; Gma<i>, G. max</i>; Jre, <i>J. regia</i>; Ppe<i>, P. persica</i>; Zju<i>, Z. jujube</i>; Mno, <i>M. notabilis;</i> Vvi, <i>V. vinifera</i>; Osa, <i>O._sativa</i> (details in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">11</a>). MRCA is the most recent common ancestor. Gene family expansions are indicated in green, and gene family contractions are indicated in orange</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Here, we report the sequencing and de novo assembly of the genome of a <i>T. sinensis</i> individual growing in the Qinling Mountains, Shaanxi Province. We used the annotated genome to determine the number of protein-coding genes in <i>Tapiscia</i>; to evaluate its genome structure, including whole-genome duplication; to evaluate its phylogenetic position; and to estimate the timing of its divergence from its most recent ancestor. An important objective of this research was to identify regions of the <i>Tapiscia</i> genome linked to sex determination and to identify and describe the expression of genes that contribute to <i>Tapisciaâs</i> unusual reproductive habit.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome sequencing, assembly, repeat sequence annotation, and gene prediction</h3><p>We sequenced a single genotype of <i>T. sinensis</i>; the source tree was a wild male individual from the Qinling Mountains, China. The input data for the whole-genome assembly included three libraries with insert sizes from 350âbp to 20âkb (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">1</a>). Most of the data were sequences from the ends of short (400âbp) fragments; 59.30âGb were sequenced from an Illumina paired-end library with an insert size of 350âbp. The sequence data represented ~131.97Ã coverage of the genome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">1</a>). We generated longer-fragment libraries of 20âkb that assisted greatly in linking contigs into scaffolds using the PacBio Sequel platform. Using the PacBio platform, we generated 39.96âGb of sequence data representing approximately 88.93Ã coverage of the genome. We also sequenced 54.93âGb of data using the 10X Genomics platform, which generates 500â700âbp read lengths. These data represented ~122.25Ã coverage of the genome. The final genome assembly was 410âMb, with contig and scaffold N50 sizes of 1.72 and 4.64âkb, respectively (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Tab1">1</a> and Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">2</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">3</a>). The final assembly size was ~96% of the estimated total genome size based on 17-mer analysis (427.77âMb) (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM1">1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM1">2</a>). A total of 154.19âGb of sequence resulted in ~343.15Ã coverage of the genome. We identified 2267 complete benchmarking universal single-copy orthologs (BUSCOs), including 2153 single-copy BUSCOs, 113 duplicated BUSCOs, 20 fragmented BUSCOs, and 37 missing BUSCOs, in the assembled <i>Tapiscia sinensis</i> genome (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Tab1">1</a>). The chloroplast genome of <i>T. sinensis</i> was assembled separately into one circular genome with a size of 161,100âbp, including a large single-copy (LSC) region of 87,766âbp, a small single-copy (SSC) region of 18,520âbp, and an inverted repeat region (IRa and IRb) of ~27,407âbp<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Ren, X. L. et al. Characterization of the complete chloroplast genome sequence of Tapiscia sinensis (Tapisciaceae). Conserv. Genet. Resour. 10, 765â768 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d27579e863">27</a></sup>. The tandem repeat (long terminal repeat, LTR) sequence annotations showed that transposable elements accounted for ~5.2% of the reference genome (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Tab1">1</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">2</a>). The GC content was ~33.41% across the <i>T. sinensis</i> genome.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Statistics and BUSCO evaluation of the <i>Tapiscia sinensis</i> genome</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Transcriptome sequencing and assembly</h3><p>We generated a total of 29.7âGb of RNA sequence data from male flowers, hermaphroditic flowers, fruits, roots, bark, leaves, young stems (tender shoots), and mature stems to annotate the genome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">4</a>). Trinity splicing results displayed 147,609 subclusters in PASA software by alignment clustering. The transcriptome sequences covered 84.91% of the reference genome. Over 456 million paired-end reads were sequenced across the six tissues. As a measure of the quality of the genome assembly of <i>T. sinensis</i>, 77.34% of 197,283 unigenes from the RNA-seq data were aligned to the genome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">5</a>). More than 80% of transcript reads could be mapped to the Illumina reads, with only slight variation based on tissue source [fruit (88.51%), root (88.10%), bark (88.42%), leaf (80.52%), young stem (90.50%), and mature stem (88.07%)] (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">4</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">5</a>). We defined 22,251 protein-coding genes (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Tab1">1</a> and Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">2</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">3</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">6</a>). Homology-based gene searches and searches for predicted noncoding RNA (ncRNA) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">2</a>) yielded 1180 ribosomal RNA (rRNA) genes, 497 transfer RNA (tRNA) genes, 383 small nuclear RNA (snRNA) genes, and 364 microRNA (miRNA) genes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">2</a>).</p><p>A total of 92,073 simple sequence repeats (SSRs) were found in <i>T. sinensis</i> (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">7</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">9</a>). We annotated 72,706 SSRs for studying the population genetics of <i>T. sinensis</i> (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">7</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">9</a>). We designed 150 pairs of primers and found 68 polymorphic loci (45.5%). Six polymorphic SSRs were used for genetic analysis of 51 <i>T. sinensis</i> individuals<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Zhou, X. J. et al. De novo characterization of flower bud transcriptomes and the development of EST-SSR markers for the endangered tree Tapiscia sinensis. Int. J. Mol. Sci. 16, 12855â12870 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d27579e1208">28</a></sup>. We used the sex-linked SSR locus TS095 (forward: TTGTCCCTCTCAACTTCGCT, reverse: AAAATCAACCAGCCAGTTCG)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Zhou, X. J. et al. De novo characterization of flower bud transcriptomes and the development of EST-SSR markers for the endangered tree Tapiscia sinensis. Int. J. Mol. Sci. 16, 12855â12870 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d27579e1212">28</a></sup> to evaluate 1041 <i>T. sinensis</i> offspring (seeds) from five locations, including the Northwest University campus, Qinling Mountains, Wuling Mountains, Daba Mountains, and Luoxiao Mountains (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">10</a>). The results based on segregation of the TS095 SSR alleles showed that the sex ratio among these 1041 offspring was nearly 1:1; the numbers of males and hermaphrodites were 505 and 536, respectively (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">10</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Comparative analysis of the <i>T. sinensis</i> genome and gene-based phylogeny</h3><p>The annotated gene models (<i>N</i>â=â22,251) were grouped into gene families (<i>N</i>â=â6,767) comprising at least two genes based on sequence similarity. We identified 229 <i>T. sinensis</i>-specific genes that did not cluster with any genes from any of 17 other plant species: <i>Morus notabilis, Ziziphus jujuba</i> (jujube), <i>Prunus persica</i> (peach), <i>Oryza sativa</i> (rice), <i>Arabidopsis thaliana, Juglans regia</i> (common walnut), <i>Vitis vinifera</i> (grape), <i>Jatropha curcas</i>, <i>Glycine max</i> (soybean), <i>Populus trichocarpa</i> (poplar), <i>Gossypium raimondii</i> (cotton), <i>Theobroma cacao</i> (cocoa), <i>Fraxinus chinensis</i>, <i>Carica papaya</i> (papaya), <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i> (tomato), <i>Olea europaea</i> and <i>Citrus sinensis</i> (orange) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">11</a>). A total of 10,733 gene families were found based on comparisons among <i>T. sinensis</i>, <i>C. sinensis</i>, <i>A. thaliana</i>, and <i>T. cacao</i>; this four-species comparison revealed 727 species-specific genes in <i>T. sinensis</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig1">1b</a> and Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">12</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">13</a>). We constructed a phylogenetic tree of <i>T. sinensis</i> together with 17 other sequenced plant genomes based on 594 single-copy genes using the monocot rice (<i>O. sativa</i>) as an outgroup (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig1">1a</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">1</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM1">3</a>). The resulting tree shows that <i>T. sinensis</i> diverged approximately 100âMa from a clade containing the Rutaceae, Brassicaceae, and Malvaceae, among others (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig1">1a</a>).</p><p>The distribution of Ks values peaked at approximately 0.34 in <i>T. sinensis</i>, indicating that <i>T. sinensis</i> has not undergone any recent lineage-specific whole-genome duplication (WGD) events (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig1">1c</a>). The distribution of 4DTv values at approximately 0.45 supported this interpretation of the fourfold degenerate site (4DTv) results (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM1">4</a>). Pairwise orthology between species, including the orthology between <i>T. sinensis</i> and <i>P. trichocarpa</i> (Salicaceae), <i>T. sinensis</i> and <i>T. cacao</i>, and <i>T. sinensis</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i>, showed 4DTv distance peaks at ~0.30, ~0.30, and 0.60, respectively, indicating that the divergence time of <i>T. sinensis</i> from <i>A. thaliana</i> was earlier than that from <i>P. trichocarpa</i>, consistent with the phylogenetic tree (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig1">1a</a>).</p><p>To identify gene families that had expanded or contracted only in <i>T. sinensis</i>, we compared gene families from <i>T. sinensis</i> with those of 17 other representative species and with an ancestral species. We identified a total of 57 gene families that have undergone significant (<i>p</i>â&lt;â0.01) expansion in the <i>T. sinensis</i> genome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">14</a>). The results of Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes (KEGG) pathway enrichment analysis revealed that these families were enriched with genes involved in phototransduction, anthocyanin biosynthesis, calcium signaling, oocyte meiosis, and plantâpathogen interaction, indicating that these pathways have evolved distinctly in <i>T. sinensis</i> compared to other plant species. Information about enrichment provides a basic resource for understanding secondary metabolism in <i>T. sinensis</i> (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">14</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">16</a>). Analysis of gene ontology (GO) and KEGG pathways showed that male and hermaphroditic plants were enriched in different annotations; for example, in males, we found that the greatest number of enriched genes were related to metabolic process, catalytic activity, and hydrolase activity, whereas enrichment in the hermaphroditic plant genome was in binding genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig4">4b</a> and Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM1">5</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM1">6</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">17</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Genetic diversity and population structure of <i>T. sinensis</i>
</h3><p>To investigate the genomic diversity, population structure, and biogeography of <i>T. sinensis</i>, we sequenced 55 trees from 10 locations that represent the speciesâ entire natural distribution in China (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig2">2a, b</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">18</a>). Thus, we generated a total of 560âGb of high-quality, cleaned sequence data, at an average of 10.19âGb per sample (equivalent to ~25Ã coverage of the ~410-Mb sequence of the wild male individual âTree168â). Sequences of Tree 168 were also mapped to the reference genome (mapping rate of 90.04%) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">19</a>). A total of 11,431,841 single nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs) were identified among 55 individuals, with an average of ~27 SNPs per kb of the <i>T. sinensis</i> genome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">19</a>). A total of 62.1% of these SNPs were intergenic; 4.2% of the SNPs were located in coding sequences (CDS), 2.12% were synonymous, and 2.06% were nonsynonymous, resulting in a nonsynonymous/synonymous ratio of 0.97 (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">20</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Spatial genetic structure of Tapiscia (Tapisciaceae)."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Spatial genetic structure of <i>Tapiscia</i> (Tapisciaceae).</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00414-w/MediaObjects/41438_2020_414_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00414-w/MediaObjects/41438_2020_414_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="685" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b> Locations of fossils of Tapisciaceae (red dots). <i>Huertea</i> is from the New World only, and <i>Tapiscia</i> is Eurasian. Current distributions of <i>Huertea</i> and <i>Tapiscia</i> are shown with gray and pink shading, respectively. <b>b</b> Spatial genetic structure of <i>Tapiscia</i> from ten sampled locations within the speciesâ natural range; pie chart colors reflect the probability of sample assignment based on independent, nonmissing SNPs analyzed using the software STRUCTURE at the most likely <i>K</i>â=â5. <b>c</b> Neighbor-joining tree based on all SNPs in 55 individuals of <i>T. sinensis</i>. Samples shaded with yellow-green were male trees, samples shaded with green were hermaphroditic trees; color-coding of the branches reflects the structure of genetic groups (see panel <b>d</b>) at <i>K</i>â=â5. <b>d</b> Results of STRUCTURE analyses of 55 individuals of <i>T. sinensis</i> at <i>K</i>â=â3â5. <b>e</b> Plot of cross-validation errors for STRUCTURE runs where <i>K</i> values ranged from 2 to 10 (optimal <i>K</i>â=â5)</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We analyzed the population structure of 55â<i>T. sinensis</i> trees using three methods: STRUCTURE runs on genomic SNPs, a neighbor-joining (NJ) tree, and a principal component analysis (PCA) of all SNPs (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig2">2bâd</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM1">7</a>). The clearest differentiation was found in the nuclear genome; five distinct groups emerged from the analysis. The populations of Zhejiang Province and the Tianmu Mountains (TM and BM, respectively, yellow in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig2">2b</a>) were the most geographically isolated and most genetically distinct (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig2">2</a>). The Yunnan Province (YN) population (southwestern China) was the second most genetically distinct. The third group was centered in the Qinling Mountains (NX and ND), the fourth population was from Guizhou Province (XF), and the fifth group of samples constituted a population in Hubei Province [(Shennongjia national forest (SN) and Lichuan County (LC)], Hunan Province (FH), and Chongqing (HS) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig2">2</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM1">7</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">18</a>).</p><p>Bayesian analysis of the genetic structure of the 55 trees from all 10 sampled sites produced populations that corresponded exactly with the clusters identified using neighbor joining (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig2">2bâd</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM1">7</a>). The underlying hierarchical structure of the <i>T. sinensis</i> trees was best represented at <i>K</i>â=â5, in part because the individuals clustered into populations that corresponded to the geographic regions from which they were sampled (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig2">2e</a>). The results of PCA revealed five clusters of samples corresponding to the same groups identified by STRUCTURE and further supported the genetic distinctiveness of populations TM and BM (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig2">2</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM1">7</a>). The distribution of <i>T. sinensis</i> in China is marked by strong fragmentation. The populations of Zhejiang, Yunnan, and Guizhou are highly differentiated genetically from those of other regions, and there was no gene flow signal into these populations from populations in other regions. Two populations of <i>T. sinensis</i> growing in the Qinling Mountains clustered into one genetic component. <i>T. sinensis</i> trees in the Shennongjia Mountains (SN) population showed evidence of gene introgression from the NX and ND populations (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig2">2</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Morphology and development of male and hermaphroditic flowers</h3><p>We compared the morphological differentiation of male versus hermaphroditic flowers (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig3">3</a>). <i>T</i>. <i>sinensis</i> flowers consist of five stamens with greenish filaments and yellow anthers 1â2âmm in length. In hermaphroditic flowers, the ovary is unilocular with 1 ovule; the style is longer than the stamens in bisexual flowers (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig3">3d, e</a>). In male flowers, the ovary is present but vestigial (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig3">3d, e</a>). Scanning electron microscope (SEM) images showed that there were no obvious differences between male flowers (MFs) and hermaphroditic flowers (HFs) before stage 5 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig3">3H, h</a>). At stage 6, however, in hermaphroditic flowers, a ring meristem forms as a ridge around the central zone of the flower (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig3">3I</a>, J). At stage 7, the gynoecium grows as a hollow tube (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig3">3</a>J); from stages 11 to 14 (during flowering), the gynoecium becomes ready for fertilization, and when the stamens extend out from the petals, fertilization occurs (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig3">3K, L</a>). In contrast, at stage 6, in male flowers, the primordium of the gynoecium begins to form as a small bulge (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig3">3i, j</a>), unlike the apex of bisexual flowers (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig3">3I</a>). Male flowers produce a solid, nonfunctional, pistil-like structure (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig3">3e, k</a>). Bisexual flowers shed spherical pollen grains with a perforate tectum (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig3">3F, G</a>), while male flowers produced truncate pollen grains with a reticulate tectum (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig3">3f, g</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Floral morphology and development of Tapiscia sinensis5,6."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Floral morphology and development of <i>Tapiscia sinensis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Ren, X. L. et al. Abnormal 1 tapetum development in hermaphrodites of an androdioecious tree, Tapiscia sinensis. Tree Physiol. 40, 108â118 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d27579e1679">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Xin, G. L. et al. Anatomy and RNA-Seq reveal important gene pathways regulating sex differentiation in a functionally Androdioecious tree, Tapiscia sinensis. BMC Plant Biol. 19, 554 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d27579e1682">6</a></sup>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00414-w/MediaObjects/41438_2020_414_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00414-w/MediaObjects/41438_2020_414_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="818" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>A</b> Hermaphroditic <i>T. sinensis</i> individual showing the simultaneous occurrence of flowers and fruit. <b>B</b> Hermaphroditic <i>T. sinensis</i> individual with fruits of the previous year and fruits of the current year developing on the same branch. <b>C</b> Male inflorescence on a male individual. <b>D</b>â<b>G</b> Hermaphroditic flowers; <b>D</b> one pistil shedding pollen and five anthers; <b>E</b> longitudinal section of a hermaphroditic flower showing the pistil and unilocular ovary; <b>F</b> spherical pollen; <b>G</b> the exine sculpture of pollen grains, showing details of the perforate tectum. <b>d</b>â<b>g</b> Male flower; <b>d</b> infertile pistil and five anthers; e longitudinal section of a male flower with no functional gynoecium; <b>f</b> olivary pollen; <b>g</b> the exine sculpture of pollen grains showing details of the reticulate tectum. <b>H</b>â<b>L</b>, <b>h</b>â<b>l</b> Scanning electron micrographs of flowers; hermaphrodite (<b>H</b>â<b>L</b>) and male (<b>h</b>â<b>l</b>) at various developmental stages; carpel primordium phase of hermaphroditic (<b>H</b>) and male (<b>h</b>) flowers. Gynoecial ridge stage of hermaphroditic (<b>I</b>, <b>J</b>) and male (<b>i</b>, <b>j</b>) flowers (note: we did not observe a gynoecial ridge stage of male pistil development; five petals were removed to make the gynoecium visible). Fully differentiated but immature hermaphroditic (<b>K</b>) and male (<b>k</b>) flowers. Mature hermaphroditic (<b>L</b>) and male (<b>l</b>) flowers. cz, Central zone; Ov, ovary; p, petal; pi, pistil; rm, ring meristem; st, stamen; vp, vestigial pistil</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Selection associated with the sex-linked genomic regions of <i>T. sinensis</i>
</h3><p>To identify sex-linked genomic regions of <i>T. sinensis</i>, we compared the genomes of 26 hermaphroditic individuals and 29 male individuals sampled from a variety of populations (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">18</a>). Using <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> values (cutoff set to 10Eâ5), we identified 303 candidate sex-linked genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig4">4a, b</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">21</a>). A high proportion (<i>N</i>â=â79, 26.1%) of these candidates were located on scaffold 25 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig4">4c</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">21</a>). The genes in males with the greatest <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> were involved in amine metabolism, catalytic activity, and hydrolase activity. In hermaphroditic plants, the genes with the greatest <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> were related to RNA binding and calcium ion binding. Other genes on scaffold 25 of hermaphroditic plants included phospholamban (<i>PLN</i>), late embryogenesis abundant (<i>LEA</i>), glyoxalase (<i>Gly</i>), major facilitator superfamily (<i>MFS</i>), ribosomal-S25 (<i>RPL</i>), 50S ribosome-binding GTPase (<i>RbgA</i>), ferredoxin (<i>Fdx</i>), and ribosomal protein l 10A (<i>RPL10A</i>) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig4">4c</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">21</a>). We chose to analyze these loci further for insights into the genomic basis of androdioecy in <i>T. sinensis</i>. Among the 303 candidate sex-linked genes, we found that 64, 157, and 77 genes were expressed at significantly different levels in male flowers, hermaphroditic flowers, and fruits, respectively (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM1">8</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">21</a>). We further found that 34 of 303 genes (11.2%) had <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> values &gt;0.2, nine of which were highly expressed in male flowers, seventeen of which were highly expressed in hermaphroditic flowers, and eight of which were highly expressed in fruits (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM1">8</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">21</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Candidate genes associated with sex-linked genomic regions of T. sinensis."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Candidate genes associated with sex-linked genomic regions of <i>T. sinensis</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00414-w/MediaObjects/41438_2020_414_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00414-w/MediaObjects/41438_2020_414_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="902" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b> Alignment of genomic regions differentially enriched in male versus hermaphroditic groups of <i>T. sinensis</i>. Concentric rings, from outermost to innermost, include âa1ââScaffolds, âa2ââSNPs, âa3ââIndels, âa4â-CNV duplications, âa5ââCNV deletions, âa6ââSV deletions, and âa7ââSV inventions. <b>b</b> The GO enrichments of genes existed only in males and hermaphrodites. <b>c</b> A Manhattan plot showing signatures of sequence divergence between male and hermaphroditic <i>T. sinensis</i>; histograms along each scaffold were based on a 20âkb window. The dots of different colors indicate regions of selective sweeps associated with sex linkage. Red arrows identify candidate genes with high and significant <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> values between the genomes of pools of male and hermaphroditic <i>T. sinensis</i>. <b>d</b> Comparison of the <i>RPL10A</i> motif composition of <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>T. sinensis</i>. <b>e</b> Gene structure of the <i>RPL10A</i> gene in male and hermaphroditic genotypes of <i>T. sinensis</i> compared with <i>A. thaliana</i>. <b>f</b> Molecular structure of <i>TsRPL10A-M</i> (male) and <i>TsRPL10A-H</i> (hermaphrodite) alleles verified by Sanger sequencing [the locations of primers (see Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">22</a>) are indicated by a light blue line (male) and light red line (hermaphroditic)]. The black line indicates variation (missing sequences) in hermaphroditic <i>T. sinensis</i>. <b>g</b> The PCR-based products of three male individuals and three hermaphroditic individuals using Sanger sequencing data. Three deletions (978âbp, 659âbp, and 773âbp) within <i>RPL10A</i> were found in hermaphroditic individuals (lanes 4 to 6, 11 to 13, and 19 to 21) compared with male individuals (lanes 1 to 3, 8 to 10, and 15 to 17). Lanes 4, 11, and 18 were controls (purified water was used as the template). <b>h</b> Tissue-specific expression of <i>RPL10A</i> genes identified in male flowers (M), hermaphroditic flowers (H), and fruits (F) of <i>T. sinensis</i> during the 8 months immediately following fertilization (for details, see Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">23</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM1">13</a>). <i>RPL10A</i> genes and their expression values across different tissues are visualized as a heatmap. Genes associated with differences between the genomes of male and hermaphroditic plants are shown as bars (details on the sampled tissues are provided in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">23</a>). <b>i</b> The mean expression of three <i>TsF-box</i> (<i>TsF-box4</i>, <i>TsF-box10</i>, and <i>TsF-box13</i>) genes identified in four male flowers (M), six hermaphroditic flowers (H), and eight fruits (F) of <i>T. sinensis</i> (for details on the sampled tissues, see Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">24</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM1">15</a>). <b>j</b> The mean expression of each of five <i>TsSUT</i> (<i>TsSUT1</i>, <i>TsSUT2</i>, <i>TsSUT3</i>, and <i>TsSUT5</i>) genes in male flowers, hermaphroditic flowers, and fruits of <i>T. sinensis</i> (for details, see Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">24</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM1">14</a>). <b>k</b> The mean expression of the <i>TsRPL10A9</i> gene in male flowers (M) versus hermaphroditic flowers (H) of <i>T. sinensis</i> (for details, see Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">23</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM1">13</a>)</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>A total of 13 <i>TsRPL10A</i> genes were identified in the genome of <i>T. sinensis</i> using a search based on the conserved domain of <i>A. thaliana RPL10A</i>. The <i>TsRPL10A</i>s were unevenly distributed among 11 scaffolds, but only <i>TsRPL10A9</i> was located on scaffold 25 of <i>T. sinensis</i>. We analyzed the conserved domains and gene structure of the ribosomal_L1 superfamily <i>RPL10A</i> gene in male and hermaphroditic plants of <i>T. sinensis</i> and compared them to those in <i>A. thaliana</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig4">4d, e</a>). The results showed that the <i>TsRPL10A9</i> gene of male <i>T. sinensis</i> was similar to the <i>A. thaliana RPL10A</i> gene; by contrast, several large deletions were present in the same locus of hermaphroditic plants of <i>T. sinensis</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig4">4d, e</a> and Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM1">9</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM1">12</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">22</a>). We verified the sequences of <i>TsRPL10A9</i> (male) and <i>TsRPL10A9</i> (hermaphroditic) by Sanger sequencing (for details of the six primer pairs used to span the gene, see Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">22</a> and Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM1">9</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM1">12</a>). We performed a sequence alignment of <i>TsRPL10A9</i> (male) and <i>TsRPL10A9</i> (hermaphrodite) with the reference genome and annotated the resulting combination with gene structure analysis (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig4">4dâg</a> and Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM1">9</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM1">12</a>). The first 2660âbp of <i>TsRPL10A9</i> were identical between male and hermaphroditic plants, and all seven coding sequences (CDS1 to CDS7) of <i>TsRPL10A9</i> were present in male trees. In contrast, there were three deletions in exons of hermaphrodites that totaled 2410âbp [978âbp, 659âbp, and 773âbp located at CDS5 (5,423,343â5,423,399), CDS6 (5,424,898â5,424,977), and CDS7 (5,425,067â5,426,207), respectively]. These deletions in hermaphroditic plants were the major sources of sequence variation in <i>TsRPL10A</i> genes between male and hermaphroditic plants (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig4">4fâh</a> and Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM1">9</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM1">12</a>).</p><p>We examined the expression of <i>TsRPL10As</i> in <i>T. sinensis</i> tissues, including male and hermaphroditic flowers (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig4">4h</a> and Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">23</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">24</a>). Interestingly, most <i>TsRPL10A</i> genes were highly expressed in <i>T. sinensis</i> tissues, including fruits, male flowers, and hermaphroditic flowers (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig4">4h</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM1">13</a>), but two <i>RPL10A</i> genes (<i>TsRPL10A4</i> and especially <i>TsRPL10A9</i>) showed higher expression in male and hermaphroditic flowers than in other tissues (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig4">4h</a>). Moreover, <i>TsRPL10A9</i> was expressed at higher levels in male flowers than in hermaphroditic flowers (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig4">4k</a>).</p><p><i>F-box</i> and sucrose transporter (<i>SUT</i>) family genes had high <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> values, and members of these gene families were also on scaffold 25. For that reason, we investigated the transcript expression patterns of <i>F-box</i> and <i>SUT</i> family genes in <i>T. sinensis</i> tissues (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig4">4i, j</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM1">14</a> and Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">23</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">24</a>). We found that three <i>F-box</i> genes (<i>TsF-box4</i>, <i>TsF-box10</i>, and <i>TsF-box13</i>) and two <i>TsSUT</i> genes (<i>TsSUT1</i> and <i>TsSUT4</i>) were differentially expressed in male versus hermaphroditic flowers of <i>T. sinensis</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig4">4j</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM1">14</a> and Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">23</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">24</a>). Nine <i>TsF-box</i> genes (<i>TsF-box</i>1, <i>TsF-box2, TsF-box5</i>, <i>TsF-box7</i>, <i>TsF-box9</i>, <i>TsF-box13</i>, <i>TsF-box14</i>, <i>TsF-box19</i>, and <i>TsF-box22</i>) contained a conserved self-incompatibility (<i>S-locus</i>) domain (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM1">15</a>), although self-incompatibility has not been investigated in <i>T. sinensis</i>. We found that the expression of <i>TsRPL10A2</i>, <i>TsRPL10A7</i>, <i>TsRPL10A11</i>, <i>TsF-box14</i>, and <i>TsSUT4</i> was low in hermaphroditic individuals at stage 6 (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM1">13</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM1">15</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Fruit quiescence in <i>T. sinensis</i>
</h3><p>The bisexual flowers of <i>T</i>. <i>sinensis</i> bloom in June, but <i>T. sinensis</i> takes 17 months to complete the cycle from floral primordium initiation (in March or April) to fruit maturation. After fertilization, the young fruit develops rapidly. The receptacle becomes fleshy and swollen, wrapping the ovary and forming a gourd-shaped structure. Young fruits become quiescent in August; from September to February, fruits are in deep quiescence (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig5">5a</a>), and their morphology and size remain unchanged until mid-April of the following year. In late March, flower development is initiated, and fruits reinitiate development; thus, flowers and fruits grow synchronously on hermaphroditic individuals (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig3">3a</a>). By the end of June, the current seasonâs fruits and previous yearâs fruits are found on the same branch (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig3">3b</a>). Two months later (mid-August), the fruits of the preceding year are fully mature, and young fruits begin their quiescent period.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="The morphology, anatomy, and gene expression levels of T. sinensis fruit during the 8 months immediately following fertilization."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: The morphology, anatomy, and gene expression levels of <i>T. sinensis</i> fruit during the 8 months immediately following fertilization.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00414-w/MediaObjects/41438_2020_414_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00414-w/MediaObjects/41438_2020_414_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="467" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p><b>a</b> The morphological characteristics of young fruits; actively growing fruits appear greenish in color, and quiescent fruits appear brown. <b>b</b> Structure of <i>T. sinensis</i> fruits over 8 months of development. <b>c</b> Tissue-specific expression of <i>LEA</i> gene family members in <i>T. sinensis</i> over 8 months (as in <b>a</b>, <b>b</b>) of fruit quiescence and development visualized as a heatmap. Transcript expression in flowers was used as a control. The fruit tissues were collected from January to August, and details are described in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">24</a>. The genes shown were among those with high <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> values between male and hermaphroditic plants, as shown in Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM1">16</a>. <b>d</b> Tissue-specific expression of <i>F-box</i> and <i>SUT</i> gene family members in <i>T. sinensis</i> over 8 months of fruit quiescence and development. an, anther; Cp, covered part of fruit; cz, central zone; ep, epidermis; mc, mucilaginous locule; Np, naked pericarp; Ov, ovary; Ph: phellem; Re: receptacle; st, stamen</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>At the time of flowering, the ovary wall is composed of an inner and outer epidermis and 6â7 layers of parenchyma cells (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig5">5b</a>). In July, the parenchyma cells under the epidermis of the ovary wall develop a cork cambium that undergoes periclinal division to form phellem and phelloderm (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig5">5b</a>). In August, the phellogen cells in the ovary wall undergo periclinal division to form 3â6 layers of cork cells (outward) and one layer of phelloderm cells (inward) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig5">5b</a>). The parts of the fruit protected by the remnant of the perianth and receptacle (âcoveredâ) do not form periderm (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig5">5b</a>). Abundant mucilage locules form in the naked pericarp and, at the same time, in the whole receptacle (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig5">5b</a>).</p><p>The molecular mechanisms underlying the regulation of <i>T. sinensis</i> fruit quiescence and embryo development are likely to be complex and strongly temporally regulated. To understand the regulation of the development of <i>T. sinensis</i> fruit, we integrated genomic and transcriptomic analyses of genes in <i>T. sinensis</i> fruit at eight stages of quiescence and development (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig5">5</a> and Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">21</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">25</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">26</a> and Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM1">16</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM1">17</a>). Of the genes with high <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> values between male and hermaphroditic plants, we focused on the <i>LEA</i> protein gene family, <i>TsF-box</i> genes, and <i>TsSUT</i> genes. Six <i>TsLEA</i> genes (<i>TsLEA2</i>, <i>TsLEA4</i>, <i>TsLEA10</i>, <i>TsLEA12</i>, <i>TsLEA13</i>, and <i>TsLEA19</i>), three <i>F-box</i> genes (<i>TsF-box5</i>, <i>TsF-box9</i>, and <i>TsF-box14</i>), and a <i>TsSUT4</i> gene were expressed at lower levels during fruit quiescence than at later times when fruits were actively developing (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig5">5c, d</a>). We found that the glycine-rich protein (<i>GRP</i>) <i>TsGRP1.8</i> gene was expressed at significantly higher levels during fruit quiescence compared with stages of active development (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig5">5b, d</a> and Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM1">16</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM1">17</a>).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Evolution of the androdioecious tree <i>T. sinensis</i>
</h3><p>The genome of the androdioecious tree <i>T. sinensis</i> was assembled and evaluated using 154âGb of clean read data (343X). The final estimated genome size was 410âMb, among the smallest reported for any tree. The genome was preliminarily annotated using RNA-seq data, from which we identified 22,251 protein-coding genes. Our study included a nearly complete representation and localization of genes, repeat elements, WGD, and RNAs; functional and metabolic annotations; and an analysis of the expression of genes with a putative role in sex determination and fruit development. A comparison of the genome of <i>T. sinensis</i> with the genomes of 17 angiosperm plants revealed differences in evolutionary expansion and contraction of gene families, in addition to unique gene families. These data represent an important resource for the study of evolutionary processes, gene evolution and function, molecular genetics and biochemistry in <i>Tapiscia</i> and the <i>Huertales</i>.</p><p>Currently, the genus <i>Tapiscia</i> comprises a single species, <i>T. sinensis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Liu, W. The <i>T. sinensis</i> habitats in the mountains of subtropical China were fragmented by past climatic changes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Zhang, J. et al. Phylogeography and genetic structure of a tertiary relict tree species, Tapiscia sinensis (Tapisciaceae): implications for conservation. Ann. Bot. 116, 727â737 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d27579e2657">25</a></sup>. Our results based on whole-genome resequencing showed that extant populations have low genetic diversity, are genetically distinct, and show little evidence of genetic exchange among populations (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig2">2</a>). The same conclusions were reached by Zhang et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Zhang, J. et al. Phylogeography and genetic structure of a tertiary relict tree species, Tapiscia sinensis (Tapisciaceae): implications for conservation. Ann. Bot. 116, 727â737 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d27579e2665">25</a></sup> based on chloroplast haplotypes. It is possible that fragmentation of <i>T. sinensis</i> populations has resulted in populations with unique adaptations (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig2">2</a>). We suggest that populations of <i>T. sinensis</i> in the Tianmu Mountains, the Qinling Mountains, the Shennong Mountains, the Naling Mountains, and Yunnan Province be given the highest priority for conservation (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig2">2</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">The transcriptomic and developmental bases of androdioecy</h3><p>Androdioecy is a rare breeding system (occurring in &lt;0.005% of angiosperms) in which populations consist of males and hermaphrodites<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Vernet, P. et al. Evidence for the long-term maintenance of a rare self-incompatibility system in Oleaceae. N. Phytol. 210, 1408â1417 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d27579e2689">10</a></sup>. The genetic and genomic bases of sex determination in plants are a critical area of study in biology and evolution. Although sex-linked systems are important in some horticultural plant species, such as red bayberry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Jia, H. M. et al. The red bayberry genome and genetic basis of sex determination. Plant. Biotechnol. J. 17, 397â409 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d27579e2693">29</a></sup>, melons<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Boualem, A. et al. A conserved mutation in an ethylene biosynthesis enzyme leads to andromonoecy in melons. Science 321, 836â838 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d27579e2697">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Martin, A. et al. A transposon-induced epigenetic change leads to sex determination in melon. Nature 461, 1135â1138 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d27579e2700">31</a></sup>, cucurbit<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Boualem, A. et al. A cucurbit androecy gene reveals how unisexual flowers develop and dioecy emerges. Science 350, 688â691 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d27579e2704">32</a></sup>, kiwifruit<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Akagi, T. et al. A Y-encoded suppressor of feminization arose via lineage-specific duplication of a cytokinin response regulator in kiwifruit. Plant Cell 30, 780â795 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d27579e2708">33</a></sup>, and persimmons (<i>OGI</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Akagi, T., Henry, I. M., Tao, R. &amp; Comai, L. A Y-chromosomeâencoded small RNA acts as a sex determinant in persimmons. Science 346, 646â650 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d27579e2716">34</a></sup>, the genomic and genetic bases of androdioecy have not been explored. We identified genomic regions and genes potentially associated with androdioecy by comparing male versus hermaphroditic genome pools. We identified a genomic scaffold (scaffold 25) that was unusually rich in candidate genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig4">4</a> and Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">18</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#MOESM2">21</a>). A 24-kb region of scaffold 25 was absent in hermaphroditic individuals. To confirm the function of this 24-kb region and determine its role in the specification of floral sexual development, a more complete, higher-quality genome assembly will be required<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Massonnet, M. et al. The genetic basis of sex determination in grapes. Nat. Commun. 11, 2902 (2020)." href="#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d27579e2729">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="MÃ¼ller, N. A. et al. A single gene underlies the dynamic evolution of poplar sex determination. Nat. Plants 6, 630â637 (2020)." href="#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d27579e2729_1">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Li, W. et al. Fine mapping of the sex locus in Salix triandra confirms a consistent sex determination mechanism in genus Salix. Hortic. Res. 7, 64 (2020)." href="#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d27579e2729_2">37</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Wang, Y. et al. Res. 7, 53 (2020)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d27579e2732">38</a></sup>. We showed that the structure and sequence of <i>RPL10A</i> differed strongly between male and hermaphroditic trees, consistent with studies showing that <i>AtRPL10A</i> mutations caused female gametophyte lethality in Arabidopsis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Imai, A., Komura, M., Kawano, E., Kuwashiro, Y. &amp; Takahashi, T. Plant J. 56, 881â890 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d27579e2743">39</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="MarÃ­a, L. F. F., Alejandro, P., Jordane, B., Alma, L. B. &amp; Paula, C. Plant L10 ribosomal proteins have different roles during development and translation under ultraviolet-B stress. Plant Physiol. 153, 1878â1894 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d27579e2746">40</a></sup>, and that there were highly significant differences in the expression patterns of candidate <i>TsF-box</i> and <i>TsSUT</i> genes in male versus hermaphroditic flowers and fruit (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig4">4</a>).</p><p>In previous studies, our results showed that bisexual flowers are the ancestral condition in <i>T. sinensis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Xin, G. Evol. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1111/jse.12517&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2019)." href="#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d27579e2764">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ren, X. L. et al. Abnormal 1 tapetum development in hermaphrodites of an androdioecious tree, Tapiscia sinensis. Tree Physiol. 40, 108â118 (2019)." href="#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d27579e2764_1">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Xin, G. BMC Plant Biol. 19, 554 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d27579e2767">6</a></sup>. Loss of female function may be the consequence of a large-scale insertion into <i>TsRPL10A</i> that caused the development of a sterile pistil in the hermaphroditic flower primordium (Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig3">3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#Fig4">4</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Imai, A., Komura, M., Kawano, E., Kuwashiro, Y. &amp; Takahashi, T. Plant J. 56, 881â890 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d27579e2780">39</a></sup>. <i>T. sinensis</i>, as an androdioecious tree, may maintain characteristics associated with self-incompatibility<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Appanah, S. Pollination of androdioecious Xerospermum intermedium Radlk.(Sapindaceae) in a rain forest. Biol. J. Linn. Soc. 18, 11â34 (1982)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d27579e2788">41</a></sup>. We identified self-incompatibility-related <i>F-box</i> genes in the <i>T. sinensis</i> genome, including <i>TsF-box14</i>, <i>TsF-box19</i>, and <i>TsF-box22</i>, which contained a conserved self-incompatibility (<i>S-locus</i>) domain<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Lai, Z. et al. Biol. 50, 29â42 (2002)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d27579e2811">42</a></sup> whose function might be confirmed by transformation into a model such as <i>A. thaliana</i> or <i>Nicotiana tabacum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Nasrallah, M. Science 297, 247â249 (2002)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d27579e2820">43</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Jane, M. et al. Expression of a Self-lncompatibility Glycoprotein (S2-Ribonuclease) from Nicotiana alata in Transgenic Nicotiana tabacum. Plant Cell 4, 1063â1074 (1992)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d27579e2823">44</a></sup>.</p><p><i>Tapiscia sinensis</i>, although mostly subtropical in its distribution, also occupies habitats where temperatures can fall as low as â20âÂ°C. Cold hardiness evolves through long-term adaptation to low-temperature environments<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Xu, C. X. Research progress on the mechanism of improving plant cold hardiness. Acta Ecol. 32, 7966â7980 (2012)." href="#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d27579e2832">45</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Cao, Q., Kong, W. &amp; Wen, P. Sin. 24, 806â811 (2004)." href="#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d27579e2832_1">46</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kalberer, S. Plant Sci. 171, 3â16 (2006)." href="#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d27579e2832_2">47</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Charrier, G., Ngao, J., Saudreau, M. &amp; AmÃ©glio, T. Plant Sci. 6, 259 (2015)." href="#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d27579e2832_3">48</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="GuÃ rdia, M. et al. Genomes 12, 83 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d27579e2835">49</a></sup>. <i>T</i>. <i>sinensis</i> flowers are fertilized in early June, the young fruits become quiescent in September, and this status is maintained until the following April. Quiescent young fruits have several characteristics that appear to be adaptations to low temperatures, including phellem formation on the ovary surface, an ovary enclosed in a receptacle, zygote quiescence, and protein, carbohydrate and lipid accumulation in the âoverwintering complexâ<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Liu, W. These developmental traits, along with androdioecy, may be adaptations to long-term climatic oscillations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Liu, W. Evol. 46, 175â182 (2008)." href="#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d27579e2852">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhou, X. Soc. 180, 504â514 (2016)." href="#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d27579e2852_1">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yang, K. et al. Bot. 120, 967â977 (2017)." href="#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d27579e2852_2">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Xin, G. Among the genes that differed most between male and bisexual flowers, several had potential roles in cellular protection under cold stress or increasing storage proteins<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Huang, Y. GigaScience 8, giz036 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00414-w#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d27579e2863">51</a></sup>. The expression levels of <i>F-box</i> genes (<i>TsF-box1, TsF-box22</i>, and <i>TsF-box13</i>) and <i>SUT</i> genes (e.g., <i>TsSUT5</i>) were higher at times, consistent with a role in fruit development and quiescence<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ferreyra, M.
